L And indeed unskilful.


M So much the more diligently must we pray to God, that he would deliver us
by his gospel from the darkness of ignorance, wherein we have walked and do
yet walk.


L We will do that if we obey his holy admonitions, which we daily hear
from the master, and often times of the preachers the administers of the word
of God.


M Behold how much the loss of my penknife has profitted us.


L For that cause I am doubly glad in your behalf, first for that I had sold it
well, then that you have gotten again that which you lost.


M I thank you my Lambert.


L To our heavenly father also be prais and thanksgiving. And now seeing you
have your penknife again, what if you did make me two or three pens of these
quils?


M Let it content you, if for the present I make one for you. Are they new?


L They are indeed new, but ready except cutting, for I made them all smooth
but now, I cut off the tail and plucked off the feathers.


M Let me see them. Truly they are very good and very fit for writing.


L Whereby know you that?


M Because they have a great, stiff, and clear stalk, for the soft and such
as have a shorter stalk are little sitting for the use of writing.


L I am glad that I bought them profitably.


M Not without cause, but for how much?


L I gave two doyts for these three, it is a small price considering the worth
of the matter.


M Of whom have you bought them?


L Of a certain pedlar.


M By the merchaunts of this city each and yet not so good is sold for two
pennies.


L And yet they dare sometimes say, that they cost them more at Lions.


M That is in a manner the custom of merchaunts. For they gain nothing except
they lie much (as Cicero says).


L But go to, that I may not longer delay, let us do what we have to do.


M I shall presently dispatch it, mark me diligently, that you may learn it
some time or other.


L I observe you with a diligent eye: but I have longer space.


M That shall be in the chamber if you will visit me.


L When?


M After the goeing sorth of the school, that is at eleven of the clock in the
morning, or four of the clock in the after noon. Now you have two pens well
fitted (if I be not deceaved) for your use. This third you shall preserve whole
for your self until an other time.


L Take it you, if you please.


M Keep it rather for your self. There are enough brought to me from home.


L Have you also good ink?


M Why ask you that?


L That you may give me a little.


M Why, have you none?


L Yea, but I can not write with it.


M What is the matter?


L Because it is too thick.


M Can you not mix it?


L I have no water.


M Mix it with wine.


L Much less.


M What if you mixed it with vinegar?


L That would make the paper fleet.


M How know you that?


L I have heard it from a certain master who taught me to write.


M But I have heard somewhat else.


L Tell it me, I pray thee.


M what will you give me?


L A good quill.


M Hear then what I have learned of a certain ussher of mine: The ink which
is thinned with vinegar is hardly wiped out.


L That may be, but in the mean time give me a little for my present use.


M Hold up your inck-horne well: I will power some in to it.


L Lo there, power in: fie! how thin it is.


M Possibly because there is not gall enough in it.


L But how pale is it?


M Use it if you pleas for such as it is; for I have no better.


L What shall I do then?


M Ah fool, can you not mix it well with your pen?


L I have mixed it enough: what can I do more?


M Power it again into mine horn.


L Hold it close to, is there enough?


M Press the cotton with your pen.


L I have pressed it so much that it is almost dry, what will it be at length?


M Good inck, or at least between both.


L The rule of mediocrity is good, as we have learned of the master. But can
any good be brought out of two evil things?


M When I shall have mixed it and powered it in again, you shall see the trial.


L I am inflamed through desire to see that.


M Reach now hither your inckhorne.


L Lo there power in oh the e is enough now: wh t wast is that? you have g^ven
me more than you have kept for your self.


M Mix it once or twice together.


L A Cook were never able to confound his pattages and sauces better together.


M Try it now at length.


L Rehearse before me some proverb that I may learn some thing in the mean
time.


M Experience (as men commonly say) is the mistres of things. Have you it? do
you understand it?


L I rehearse it sooner.


M To wit, because you could say it before.


L Who should not know that, which is so common?


M Let us see now.


L The matter will appear better when the writing is dry.


M Wherefore will you tarry? It is now more than dry enough.


L Ho! see how black it is.


M Have not I spoken true?


L You bad tried it at some time or other.


M It will be then sure that experience is the mistres of things.


L By this we also find, that by mixeing things together a good medicrity is
made.


M Now you begin to philosophate higher, therefore I Will be gone. For we have
written long enough. I must go to my book.


L That is well said: let us go and repeat together.


M What should I repeat?


L That which the master has this day written out.


M Is it not sufficient that I have repeated allone?


L If you have repeated but once or twice, it is little to learn without book.


M Yea, I have repeated near ten times.


L And yet that is not enough.


M What will you more then?


L If you will say your lessen perfectly to the master, it is necessary that
you have repeated with some body?


M I knew not that: but I assent willingly to you. Beg in you then who have
admonished me.


L Go to, attend diligently, and suffer me not to miss.


M I am more ready to bear than you are to pronounce.


L But if the observatour comes in the mean time, he will think that we prate.


M why do you sear where no fear is? If he comes he shall not find us in
idleness or a bad matter: let him if he will hear our discourse.


L You speak very well: let us sit aside somewhere in a corner, that no body
may hinder us.


M But I fear that it is too late for us to benefit our selves by so doing,
for we must presently say our lessen to the master.


L For your good company, and all your good deeds to me I give you as great
thanks as I can. Fare well.


M God preserve you in health. But hear, never spare my labor.


L And do you use me and my things again, if there be any need. Again fare
well.


4 School-praetghe wegens schrijven, lessen, end 't gene daer aen behoort.
L GHy maeckt goede letteren / end schrijft heel treffelijck maer schrijft ghy
nu met eenst / of beufelt ghy?


M Voorwaer ick schrijf ernstelijck / want waerom soude ic de tijt misbruycken?
Maer ghy / waerom vraeght ghy dat?


L Om dat ick eens ghesien heb / dat ghy beter schreeft / end daerom ben ick
verwondert / hot het komt / dat ghy nu qualijcker schrijft.


M Dat is geen wonder waerdich / want my ontbreecken de behulpfelen om well te
schrijven.


L Welcke zijnse?


M Goedt pampier / goeden inckt / even goede pen: want dit mijn pampier
(ghelijck ghy siet) slaet ellendigh deur den inckt is waterich end witachtich
/ de pen is weeck end qualijck bereydt: voorders heb ick oock mijn voorschrift
verloren.


L Waerom hebt ghy all dese dinghen niet in tijts voorsien?


M Het gelt ontbrack my / end dat ontbreeckt my nu oock.


L Ghy zijt in dat ghemeene spreeck-woordt vervallen / die ghelt ontbreect
ontbreecken all dingen.


M So gaet het met my.


L Maer wanneer hoopt ghy dat ghy het su^t krijgen?


M Op de toecomende merct sal het mijn Vader my senden of hy sal selver komen.


L Ick will u ondertusschen helpen.


M So ghy dat vermeucht / ghy sult my even groote weldaet gedaen hebben.


L Neemt dese ses sluyvers te leenom pampier end andere dinghen te versorghen.


M Hoe waerlijck is dat gheseyt / Even seecker vrient werdt in even onsekere
saeck gesien? Maer wat hebt ghy in u sin dat ghy my van selfs so well doet?


L De liefde Godts / de welcke (gelijck Paulus seyt) in onse herten uytghestort
is.


M De macht des Goddelijcken Gheests is wonderlijck in onse herten die de
autheur van die liefde is. Maer ick moet ondertusschen over leggen hoe ic u
dancbaer falsijn.


L 'T is even kleyne saeck: Laet die gedachten varen / op dat sy u niet en belet
/ dat ghy te geruster slaept; gheeft my maer weder't geen ick u gheleent heb
als 't u te pas komt.


M Ick sal't so ic hoop u eerst daegs weder gheven.


L Maer wat seght ghy van het pennemes dat ick u eergisteren verkocht had?
Is It niet goed ghenoeg?


M Iae het is seer goed / maer (my ellendighe) het iffer^ wernich afgheweest of
ick had het verloren.


L Hoe / wat seght ghy? hoe is dat ghebeurt?


M Als ick buyten quam / was het my ontvallen op de straet.


L Van waer was het u ontvallen?


M With mijn koocker / die ick onversiens open ghelaten had.


L Hoe hebt ghy 't weder gecreghen?


M Ick had terstont even briefken aen de poorten van de deur ghehecht: nae het
noenmael heeft even jonghen uyt het seste school het my wedergebrocht.


L Och of sy all te samen so getrou waren / die verloren dinghen vinden.


M Voorwaer daer zijnder weynich die wedergheven / indien de saeck maer van
eenighe waerde is.


L End dat wordt nochtans door 't word Godts bynamen geboden.


M Waerom niet? want het is even manier van dieverije so yemandt even ander man's
saeck die gevonden is / houdt / so hy maer weet / wien hy het wedergeven moet.


L Maer vee^e meynen dat sy met recht besitten / al wat sy ghevonden hebben dat
verloren is.


M Die dwaelen seer swaerlijck.


L Maer (op dat wy wederkeeren tot onse begonnen reden) wat hebt ghy de jonghen
gegeven die u pennemes gevonden had?


M Ick heb hem even oortgen ghegheven end eenighe ocker-nooten: ick heb hem
daer en boven ghepresen / end met weynigh woorden hebbe ick hem vermaendt dat
hy het selve altijdt most done.


L Ghy hebt well ghedaen / want so sal hy op even ander tijdt te liever
wedergheven / so hy yets ghevonden heeft. Maer indien ghy het verloren had?


M Ick soude het met even goet gemoet verdragen hebben / end ick soude even
ander ghekocht hebben.


L Soud ghy het also met even goet ghemoet verdraghen hebben?


M Voorseeker niet sonder eenighe moeyelijckheyt.


L Niet dan niet even goet gemoet / maer ick will u niet nauwer dringhen


M Wy zijn gheen Godts geleerde.


L Wat dan?


M Grammaticas jonghens.


L End oock on ervaren.


M So veel te neerstigher moeten wy Godt bidden / op dat hy door sijn
Evangelie ons verlosse van de duysternissen der onwetentheydt in de welcke wy
berkeert hebben / end noch verkeeren.


L Dat sullen wy done so wy sijne Heylighe vermaninghen ghehoorsamen / die wy
dagelijcx hooren van de meester / end dickmaels vande predicanten bedienaers
des Goddelijcken woordts.


M Siet hoe veel ons de verliesinghe van mijn pennemes gebaet heeft.


L Om die oorsaeck ben ick dubbelt van uwent weghen bly / eerstelijck om dat
ick het well verrocht had / daer nae om dat ghy 't gheen verloren was weder
gekreghen hebt.


M Ic danc u mijn Lambertus.


L Oock zy onsen Hemelschen Vader lof end dancksegginge. End nu dewijle ghy
uwe pennemes weder hebt / wat so ghy my twee of dry pennen van dese schaffen
versneet?


M Laet het u genoech zijn / so icker even voor teghenwoordich sal versneden
hebben / zijn se ooc nieuw?


L Sy zijn well nieuw / maer veerdich als sy versneeden zijn / want ick hebse nu
al glat ghemaeckt / ick heb de steert afghesneden / ick heb de veerkens
afghetrocken.


M Laet sien, voorwaer sy fijn seer goet end tot het schrijven seer bequaem.


L Van waer weet ghy dat?


M Om dat sy even groote stijve end heldere steel hebben / want de weecke end
die even korter steel hebben zijn weynigh tot het ghebruyck van schrijven be
quaem.


L Ic ben blijde dat icse profijtelijck ghekocht hebbe.


M Niet sonder oorsaeck / maer hoe veel?


L Voor dese dry heb ick twee duyten gegeven / het is even kleyn prijs nae des
saeckx waerde.


M Van wien hebt ghyse ghekocht?


L Van even seecker kramer.


M By de coopluyden van dese Stad were elck eu^ noch so goet niet / voor twee
penninghen verkocht.


L End nochtans durven sy somtijts segghen / datse haer te Lions mere kosten.


M Dat is by na de gewoonte der coop uyden / want sy done gheen profijt / ten
sy sy seer lieghen (ghelijck Cicero seyt).


L Maer well aen / op dat ick niet langer en vertoeve / laet ons done dat voor
handen is.


M Ic sal't haest beschict hebben siet my naerstich aen / op dat ghy't d'een
tijdt of d'ander eeren mogen.


L Irk sit u aen met opmerckende ooghen: maer ick heb langer tijdt.


M Dat sal inde kamer geschieden so ghy my wilt besoecken.


L Wanneer?


M Nae het uytgaen van de School; dat is s^morghens ten elf uren / often vier
uren nae de middagh. Na hebt ghy twee pennen / (ten sy ick bedrogen ben) tot u
ghebruyck well bereyt. Dese derde sult ghy tot op even ander tijt heel voor u
bewaren.


L Neemt ghy het / so 't u belieft.


M Bewaert het liever voor u selven / daer werdender my ghenoech van huys
gebracht.


L Hebt ghy oock goeden inckt?


M Waerom vraeght ghy dat?


L Op dat ghy my even weynighsken geeft.


M Hoe / en hebt ghy geen?


L Iae / maer ick en kan daer niet me schrijven.


M Wat schorter aen?


L Om dat het al te dick is.


M Kunt ghy het niet mengen?


L Ick heb geen water.


M Menght het met wijn.


L Veel minder.


M Of ghy het met edick menghde?


L Daer soud het pampier van deur slaen.


M Hoe weet ghy dat?


L Ick heb dat ghehoort van even seeker meester / die my leerde schrijven.


M Maer ick heb wat anders ghehoort.


L Ey lieve vertelt het my.


M Wat sult ghy my gheven?


L Even goede schaft.


M Hoort dan wat ick van even seecker onder-meester van my gheleert hebbe: Den
inckt die met edick dun ghemaeckt is / were nauwelijcx uyt ghebeeght.


L Het kan gheschieden / maer onder tusschen gheeft my even weynich voor mijn
teghen-woordich ghebruyck.


M Houdt u inckt-koocker te deghen op: ick fall u daer in gieten.


L Siet daer / giet in / feye^ hoe dun isse?


M Moghelijck om datter niet gals genoegh by is.


L Maer hoe bleeck:


M Ghebruyckt het so ghy wilt voor het geene dat het is / want ick en heb geen
beeter.


L Wat sal ick dan done?


M Ho beuselaer / kunt ghy het met u pen niet well menghen?


L Ick heb het ghenoegh gemenght: wat soude ick mere konnen?


M Giedt het weder in mijn hoorn.


L Houdt het dicht aen / isse^ ghenoech?


M Druckt de boom wol met u pen.


L Ick hebt so ghedruckt dat het by nae droog zy / wat sal 't eyndelijck wesen?


M Goeden inckt / often ten minsten middel-matighe.


L Den regel van middelmatigherdt is goet ghelijck wy van de meester gheleert
hebben. Maer kan daer uyt twee quade saecken yets goets ghemaeckt werden?


M Als ick het ghemenght end wederinghegooten sal hebben / sult ghy de proef
sien.


L Ick ben vierich door verlanghen om dat te sien.


M Langht nu hier u'incktkooker.


L Siet daer / giet in. Oh it is nu ghenoegh: wat is dat voor even verquistinghe?
Ghy hebt my mere gheghoven als ghy voor u selfs behouden hebt.


M Menght het eens of twee mael t'samen.


L Even kock en soude nimmermeer sijn soppen end saussen beter konnen t'samen
storten.


M Versoeckt het nu eyndelijc.


L Verhaelt my eenige spreucken voor / op dat ick ondertusschen wat leere.


M De eervarentheyt (gelijck men gemeenelijcke seyt) is de meestersse der
dinghen. Hebt ghy't? verstaet ghy 't?


L Ick segh het rasser.


M Namenslijck om dat ghy het te voren kont.


L Wie sou dat niet weeten / dat so ghemeen is?


M Laet ons nu sien.


L De saeck sal beter blijcken als het schrift gedroogt sal zijn.


M Wat wilt ghy wachten? het is nu mere als droogh ghenoech.


L Ohe siet hoe swart dat het is?


M Heb ick niet waer geseyt?


L Gh^ had het al op d'een of d'ander tijt versocht.


M Het sal dan seecker zijn dat de ervarentheydt even meestersse der dinghen is.


L Hier door proeven wy oock dat u^t de t'samen menginghe der dinghen even goede
middel maticheydt ghemaeckt wordt.


M Nu begint ghy hooger te philosopheren / derhalven gae ick wegh. Want it is
langh ghenoech gheschreven. Ick moet aen mijn boeck gaen.


L Dat is well gheseydt: laet ons to samen gaen verhaelen.


M Wat soude ick verhaelen?


L 'T gene heden van de meester is voorgeschreven.


M En is it niet ghenoech / dat ic alleen verhaelt heb?


L So ghy maer eens of tweemael verhaelt hebt dat is weynigh om van buyten te
leeren.


M Iae ick heb by nae thien mael verhaelt.


L En dat is noch niet ghenoegh.


M Wat wilt ghy dan mere?


L So ghy tegens de meester seer sekerlijck wilt opseggen / so is it van noode /
dat ghy met yemant verhaelt hebt.


M Dat en wist ick niet maer ick s^aet u gaerne toe. Begint ghy dan die my
vermaent hebt.


L Well aen letter op / end en laet niet toe / dat ick afdwale.


M Ick ben veerdigher om te hooren / als ghy om te spreecken.


L Maer indien ondertusschen de toesiender quame / hy soude meynen dat wy
klapten.


M Wat vreest ghy daer niet te breesen is? so hy komt hy sal ons niet in
leedicheydt often in even quade faeck bevinden laet hem onse to samen sprekinghe
hooren so het hem belieft.


L Ghy spreeckt seer well: laet ons elders in even hoeck af schicken op dat ons
niemant en belette.


M Maer ick vrese dat het te laet is om ons so doende te behelpen / want wy
moeten stracx aen de meester op seggen.


L Ick weet u voor u goedt gheselschap end all'u weldaeden aen my / so
grooten danck als ick kan. vaertwel.


M Godt spare u ghesondt. Maer hoort / en spaert nimmermeer mijn ar: beydt.


L Ghy oock / ghebruyckt my end mijn dinghen wederom / so der yets van noode
sal zijn. wederom vaert well.


5 A child relates orderly to his ussher what he and his school-fellowes have
don in their riseing up, clothing them, and other things. wherein the names of
many things belonging to the body are spoken of. After that they discourse of
divine matters. A sermon is heard but nothing remembered of it.
V AT what hour were you awakened this morning?


S Before day, but at what hour I know not.


V Who awakened you?


S The weekly awaker came with his lantern; knocked hard at the chamber
door, some body opened (to him), the awaker lighted our candle, called out
with a loud voice, all were awakened.


V Relate to me orderly what you have don from that time until the end of
breakfast. You children attend diligently with ears and hearts, that you may
learn to imitate this your schoole-fellow.


S Being awakened I arose out of my bed, I put on my coat with my dublet, I
sat me down on a footbench, I got my breeches and my stockens, I put them
both on, I put on my shoes, I made fast my breeches to my dublet with points,
gattered my stockens above my knees. I put on my girdle, combed my head
diligently, I put my hat on mine head I put on my gown, after that being gone
cut of the chamber, I went down, I made water in the yard against the wall, I
got cold water out of the paile, washed both mine hands and face, rinzed my
mouth and teeth, I wiped mine hands and face with a towell, in the mean time
the token to prayer was given with the little bell, they came into the private
hall (parlour) we prayed together, we received our breakfast orderly from the
servant of the kitchen (Cook), we breakfasted sitting at the hearth, and that
still, without murmuring, without noise: Such as I heard prating unseemly, or
speaking idle words, or have seen wanton, I admonished friendly, and such as
were not obedient to my admonition I brought them to the observatour that he
might note them.


V Did no body oversee you, while you were at breakfast


S Yea, the uss^er.


V What did he in the mean time?


S He walked along in the midst of the hall, holding a book in his hands, and
some times exhorting the observatour, that he should note those that did prate
unseemly.


V Is it not then permitted to let fall a word?


S Yea, it is permitted, but they use only to be noted, who prate long and with
many words unseemly and without any fruit. But it is lawful for every one
among themselves to hold pleasant discourses of good and honest matters, yet
so as it be don quietly, without crying out and brawling.


V You have satisfied me hitherto. But you, have you pronounced already the
context against to morrow morning?


S I have repeated it.


V Well enough?


S Enough God be thanked.


V Who has heard you?


S The master.


V It Is well^ but there is some thing which I will admonish you.


S I desire much to hear that.


V You must often meditate, how much you are indebted to God the giver of al
things who has given you understanding and such an happy memory.


S what am I not indebted to him, who has given me all things?


V Tell some of his espetiall benefits, as I have sometime taught you.


S That heavenly father has given me a body, a soul, life, a good mind, good
parents, rich, noble, well affected to me; and who do not only abundantly
supply me with all things needful for this life: but also (which is the
greatest of all by far) take care to have me so diligently instructed in good
languages and good manners, that there is no more to be desired.


V You have said al those things truly: but you have omitted one thing, which
is an espetiall blessing of God. Do you know what it is?


S Let me meditate a little.


V Meditate at leisure.


S Now I remember it, but by reason of the greatness of the matter, I know not
with what words I shall express it.


V Say it nevertheles in so good a manner as you can.


S I bethink me more and more.


V Speak it at last.


S Innumerable are the benefits of the most great and good God toward me in my
body and soul in external things; but there can be no greater said or
thought, than that of his grace he has given me his only son, who has
redeemed me most miserable sinner, and captivated under the tyranie of Satan,
and that by his death (which was) of all (deaths) the most cruel and shameful.


V You have said it well enough, and very near just with so many words, as I
have taught you at other times. But has God don those so great benefits to you
only?


S No truly.


V To whom more?


S To all as many as God out of his pure grace has chosen to eternal life in
Christ, and accordingly do faithfully and truly believe the gospel.


V Go to, produce a place out of the gospel of John to that purpose.


S God so loved the world, that he gave his only begotten son: that every one
that believes in him should not perrish, but have everlasting life. For God
sent not his son into the world, that he should condemn the world, but that
the world through him might be saved. He that believes in him is not
condemned* but he that be^eev th not is condemned already, because he has not
believed in the name of his only begotten son.


V Hitherto enough. But whose words are they?


S Christ's himself, speaking of himself.


V To whom does he speak?


S Nicodemus, who came to him by night.


V Christ himself our only Savior grant, that you may grow more and more in
the knowledge of him.


S He will do it, I hope.


V Go forward then cheerfully, as you have begun, which I pray God prosper to
the glory of his name.


S That is my desire.


V Let us go to supper. But in the mean time let me ask you one thing more.
Have you not bin to day at the holy sermon?


S I was there.


V Who are your witnesses?


S Many of the schoole-fellowes who saw me can witness it.


V But some must be produced.


S I shall produce them when you command it.


V Who did preach?


S Master N.


V At what time began he?


S At seven a clock.


V Whence did he take his text?


S Out of the epistle of Paul to the Romans.


V In what chapter?


S In the eighth.


V Hitherto you have answered well: let us now see what follows. Have you
remembered any thing?


S Nothing that I can repeat.


V Nothing at al? Bethink (your self) a little, and take heed that you be not
disturbed, but be of good courage.


S Truly master I can remember nothing.


V What, not one word?


S None at all.


V I am ready to strike you, what profit have you then gotten?


S I know not, otherwise than that perhaps I have in the mean time abstained
from evil.


V That is some what indeed, if it could but so be that you have kept your self
wholly from evil.


S I have abstained so much as I was able.


G Grant that it be so, yet you have not pleased God, senig it is written,
depart from evil and do good, but tell me (I pray thee) for what cause
principally did you go thither?


S That I might learn something.


V Why have you not don so?


S I could not.


V Could you not knave? yea you would not, or truly you have not addicted your
self to it.


S I am compelled to confess it.


V What compels you?


S My Conscience, which accuses me before God.


V You say well: oh that it were from the heart.


S Truly I speak it from mine heart.


V It may be so: but go to, what was the cause that you have remembered nothing?


S My negligence: for I attended not diligently.


V What did you then?


S Sometimes I slept.


V So you use to do: but what did you the rest of the time?


S I thought on a thousand fooleries, as children are wont to do.


V Are you so very a child, that you ought not to be attentive to hear the
word of God?


S If I had bin attentive, I should have profitted something.


V What have you then meritted?


S Stripes.


V You have truly meritted them, and that very many.


S I ingenuously confess it.


V But in word only I think.


S Yea truly from mine heart.


V Possibly, but in the mean time prepare to receave stripes.


S O master forgive it, I beseech you, I confess I have sinned, but not of
malice.


V But such an evil negligence comes very near wickedness (malice).


S Truly I strive not against that: But nevertheles I implore your clemency
through Jesus Christ.


V What will you then do, if I shall forgive you?


S I will do my duty henceforth, as I hope.


V You should have added thereto, by Gods help: but you care little for that.


S Yea master, by Gods help, I will hereafter do my duty.


V Go to, I pardon you the fault for your tears: and I forgive it you on this
condition, that you be myndful of your promise.


S I thank you most Courteous master.


V You shall be in very great favor with me, if you remember your promise.


S The most good and great God grant that I may.


V That is my desire, that he would grant it.


5 Even kindt verteldt ordentlijck aen sijn ondermeester al wat hy end sijn
mede-ghesellen gedaen hebben in haren opstaen, bekleedinghe, end andere
dinghen. waerinne van de namen van veele dingen het lijf aen gaende gesproken
zijn. Daerna spreken sy van Goddelijcke saecken. Even predicatie wordt ghehoort:
maer niets daer van onthouden.
V OP wat ure sijt ghy huyden morghen opgheweckt?


S Voor den dagh / maer wat uyr en weet ick niet.


V Wie heeft u opgeweckt?


S Den weeckelijcken opwecker is met sijn lanteern gekomen / heeft heart op de
deur van de kamer geklopt / yemant heeft opgedaen / den opwecker heeft onsen
keers opgesteeken / heeft met even verheven stem uytgeroepen / altemael werden
opgheweckt.


D Vertelt my ordentlijck wat ghy gedaen hebt van die tijt af tot het eynde des
ontbijtens. Ghy kinderen hoort met ooren end herten naerstelijck toe / op dat
ghy lieden leert dese uwen medediscipel na te volghen.


S Opgheweckt zijnde ben ick uyt het bed opghestaen / ic heb mijn rock
aengetrocken met mijn wambuys ick heb geseten op even bancxkenic heb mijn broec
end mijn koussen gecregen ick hebse beyde aengetrocken / ic heb mijn schoenen
geschoeyt ick heb mijn broeck aen mijn wambuys met nesi elenvast gemaect / mijn
koussens boven mijn knien gebonden / ick heb mijn gordeles omgedaen mijn hooft
naerstelijck gekemt / ic heb mijn hoetgen op mijn hooft gevoeght / ic heb mijn
tabbert aengedaen / daerna uyt de kamer ghegaen zijnde / ben ick nae beneden
gegaen / ic heb mijn water op de plaets tegen de muer gemaeckt / ick heb coudt
water uyt den emmer ghekreghen bey mijn handen end aensight ghewasschen / mijn
mondt end tanden ghespoelt / ick heb mijn handen end aensicht met even
droogh-doeck afgewist / ondertusschen wort het teecken om te bidden gegeven met
het kleyne klocxken / men komt op de bysondert zael / wy bidden to samen / wy
krijgen ordentelijc onse ontbyten van de keucken knecht / wy ontbeetenaen den
heert sittende end dat stille / sonder gherurs / sonder geraes: die ick
gehoort hebbe onbequamelijck klappen / often leedighe woorden spreken / often
oock dertel gesien heb / die heb ick vriendelijck vermaent / end die mijn
vermaninghe niet hebben ghehoorsaem gheweest / heb ick aenghebrocht by den
opsiender op dat hy haer teyckenen soude.


V En was niemant ober u. l. done ghy ontbeet?


S Iae de ondermeester.


V Wat deed' hy ondertusschen?


S Hy wandelde midden over de zael / houdende even boeck in de handen / end
somtijdts en toesiender vermanende / dat hy die onbequamelijck klapten soude
teyckenen.


V Is het dan niet geoorlooft even word uyt te laeten gaen?


S Ia / het is geoorlooft / maer die pleghen alleenelijck geteyckent te worden
/ die langh end met veele woorden onbequamelijck end sonder eenighe vrucht
klappen. maer het is even yder geoor'ooft onder malekanderen gheneugelijcke
redenen te handelen van goede end eerlijcke saken / so nochtans dat het
stillekens gheschiet / sonder gheroep end kevagie.


V Tot hier toe hebt ghy my vo daen. Maer ghy / hebt ghy nu al den inhout tegen
morghen vroegh opghese^de?


S Ick heb het opgheseydt.


V Te deegh ghenoegh?


S Genoe ^h Godt zy danck.


V Wie heeft u ghehoort?


S De meester.


V 'T is well / maer daer is yets dat ic u wild vermanen.


S Ick begeere seer dat te hooren.


V Ghy moet dickwils dencken / hoe veel dat ghy Godt den verleener van all
dinghen schuldigh zijt / die u verstant end so gheluckighen memory ghegheven
heeft.


S Wat sal ick hem niet schuldich zijn / die m^ all dingen ghegeven heeft?


V Verhaelt eenighe van sijn voornaemste weldaden / ghelijck ick u t'eenigher
tijd gheleert hebbe.


S Dien hemelschen Vader heeft my ghegeven even lichaem / even ziel / het leven /
even goeden sin / goede ouders / rijcke / edele / well tot my genegen; end die
my niet alleen overbloede^ijck all dingen noodi^htot het leven beschaffen:
maer oock (het welcke verre het aldergrootste is) my in goede talen / end
goede manieren versorghen so naerstelijck onderwesen te werden / datter niet
mere te vereyschen zy.


V Ghy hebt all die dinghen waerlijck gheseyt: maer ghy hebt even dingh achter
gela^en / het welcke even besondere weldaet Gods is weet ghy wat het zy?


S Laet my even weynigh bedencken.


D Denckt met ghemack.


S Nu gedenck ick het / maer door de groot heydt van de saeck en weet ick niet
/ met wat woorden dat it dat uytdrucken sall.


V Seght het nochtans op sodani en manier als ghy k^unt.


S Ick dencke voort end voort.


V Seght het eyndelijck.


S On tallijck zijn des seer goeden end grooten Gods we daden tegens my in
mijn 'ichaem / in mijn ziel in de uyterlijcke din hen; maer daer kander gheen
grooter gheseyt noch ghedacht worden / dan dat hy sijnen eenighen Sone uyt
ghenade m^ ghegheven heeft die my seer ellendighe sondaer end ghevanghen onder
de tyranny des Satan's / verlost heeft / end dat door sijn dood van all de
wreetste end schandelijckste.


V Ghy hebt het bequaem genoegh gheseydt / end by nae met even so veel
woorden / als ick u 't anderen tijden gheleert had. Maer heeft Godt u alleen
die so grooten weldaet bewesen?


S Geensins voorwaer.


V Wie daer en boven?


S Allen so veel alsser Godt uyt sijn loutere genade uytverkoren heeft tot het
eeuwighe leven in Christo / end nadien het Evange'ium getrouwelijck end
waer^ijck gelooven.


V Well aen brenght voort even plaets uyt het Evangelium Iohannis op die sin.


S Also heeft Godt de werelt lief gehadt dat hy sijnen eenigen gebooren soon
gegeven heeft: op dat all die in hem ghelooft niet verloren en gaen / maer het
eeuwige leven hebbe, want Godt heeft sijnen soon niet in de werelt gesonden op
dat hy de werelt verdoeme / maer op dat de wereldt behouden word door hem. Die
in hem ghelooft en wort niet verdoemt: Maer die niet en gelooft die is nu
alreede verdoemt / om dat hy niet ghelooft en heeft inden name van sijn
eenighen geboren soon.


V Tot hier toe genoegh. Maer wiens woorden zijn die?


S Christi selfs van sich selven spreeckende.


V Wien spreeckt hy aen?


S Nicodemus / die tot hem in de nacht ghekomen was.


V Christus selfs onsen eenige Salighmaker maect, dat ghy mere end mere in
sijn kennisse toeneemt.


S Hy sal't done / so ick hoop.


V Gaet dan ghelijck ghy begonnen hebt wackerlijc voort / het welcke Godt ter
eeren sijns naems well wildoen gelucken.


S So bid ick.


V Laet ons gaen om het avonsmael te houden, maer ondertusschen laet my u. l.
noch even dinghe vraghen, zijt ghy huyden niet in de He^lige predicatie gevreest?


S I^k bender gheweest.


V Wie sijn u ghetu^ghen?


S Veyle van de mede d^scipulen / die my gesien hebben / konnen 'tgetuygen


V Mere men moeter eenighe voortbrengen.


S Ick false voortbrenghen / als ghy het fult ghebieden.


V Wie heefter ghepreeckt?


S Den Here N.


V Wat u^r heeft hy begonnen?


S Te sevenen.


V Waer uyt heeft hy sijn text ghenomen?


S With ven brief Pault tot den Romeyren.


V In het hoe heel^^e capittel?


S In het ach^ste.


V Tot noch we hebt ghy well geantwoo^^: lae^ ons nu sien warter volicht. Hebt
ghy ^ets onthouden?


S Niets dat it vertellen kan.


V Niet met al? Bedenckt even weynigh end siet toe dat ghy niet ontroert en
wort / maer zijt goets moets.


S Voorwaer meester ick en kan niets bedencken.


V Niet even word?


S Gantsch niet.


V Ick slae by kans: wat voordeel hebt ghy dan ghedaen?


S Ick en weet niet / dan dat ick misschien ondertusschen van quaedt my
onthouden heb.


V Dat is well wat / so het maer heeft konnen geschieden / dat ghy gheheel van
't quaet u hebt onthouden.


S Ick heb my onthouden so veel als ick heb ghekonnen.


V Seght dat het so zy / ghy en hebt nochtans Godt niet voldaen / dewijl daer
gheschreven staet / wijckt af van het quaede end doet 't goede: maer seght my
(bid ick) om wat oorsaeck waert ghy daer voornamentlij^k a hegaen?


S Op dat ick yets soude leeren.


V Waerom en hebt ghy dat niet ghedaen?


S Ick heb niet ghekonnen.


V Hebt ghy niet ghekonnen boef? jae ghy hebt niet ghewilt / often voorseker gh^
hebter u niet nae geschickt.


S Ick werde het gedwongen te bekennen.


V Wat dwinght u?


S Mijn ghewisse / die my voor Godt beschuldicht.


V Ghy seght well: och of 't van herten waer.


S Voorwaer ick segh't van herten.


V Het kan geschieden: maer well aen / wat is d'oorsaeck gheweest / waerom ghy
niets onthouden hebt?


S Mijn onachtsaemheydt: want ick hoorde niet naerstich toe.


V Wat deed' ghy dan?


S Somtijts sliep ick.


V So pleeght ghy: maer wat deed' ghy in de overige tijdt?


S Ick docht om duysent beuselinghen / ghelijck de kinderen pleghen.


V Zijt ghy so seer even kint / dat ghy niet en behoorde aendachtigh te wesen
om Gods word te hooren?


S So ick aendachtich ware geweest / ick soude yets konnen voorderen hebben.


V Wat hebt ghy dan / ve^dient?


S Slaghen.


V Voorwaer ghy hebtse verdient / end dat vry wat veel.


S Ick beken het vryelijck.


V Alleenlijck met woorden mien ick.


S Iae voorwaer van harten.


V Moghelijck / maer ondertusschen maect u bereyt om slaghen te ontfangen.


S O meester vergheeft het / bid ick / ick heb ghesondigt ick bekent / maer uyt
geen quaetheyt.


V Maer so even verkeerde onachtsaemheydt komt seer na by de boysheyt.


S Voorwaer ick en siry daer niet tegen: Maer nochtans hid ic om u
goedertierenheyt door Iesum Christum.


V Wat sult ghy dan done / indien ick het u vergheven sal.


S Ick sal hier na mijn ampt done / so ick hoop.


V Men behoorde daer by gedaen te hebben met Godts hulpe: maer daer vraeght ghy
weynigh nae.


S Iae meester / met Gods hulp / sal ick hier nae mijn ampt done.


V Well aen / ick vergeef y schult door uwe tranen: end ick vergeef het u op
dese conditie / dat ghy u beloften gedachtigh zijt.


S Ick bedanck u seer beleefde meester.


V Ghy sult by my in even seer grooten aenghenaemheyt zijn / indien ghy uwe
beloften gedencken sult.


S De seer goede end groote Godt maeckt dat ick kan.


V Dat hy het maeckt / dat wensch ick.


6 Certain short dialogues wherein Scholars ask their master leave to go
forth on several occasions.
S AM I permitted to go out with my Brother?


M What is the occasion?


S That our mother may buy us shoes: afterward that we may go to the barbers.


M What to do there?


S To cut off our hair.


M What need is there of yt now?


S That to morrow we may go and visit our uncle if the Lord permit.


M Go, and betimes in the morning bring me a testimony or witness with you.


S I will d^ligently take care of that by God's help. Will you have any thing
more Sir (master)?


M That you remember my service to your mother.


Another.
S MAster, may I have leave to go forth?


M Whither.


S First to the tailor, then to the barber.


M Why to the tailor?


S That I may put my hose to be amended.


M Are they torn?


S Yea so torn that I can hardly draw them on.


M Wherefore to the barber?


S That I may show him a sore which is risen this day on my thigh.


M Uncover it, that I may see it.


S See it when you please.


M It is a byle.


S I gessed it so.


M When you show it to the barber, desire him to lay a fit plaster thereon.


S I will follow your Counsel.


M But is there no body that will go with you?


S Yes, John Fluvian.


M What has he to do?


S He will go to the barber also.


M Go then together, and return in like manner.


S Will you have any thing else?


M That you make have to come again, that you lose not your after-noons
drinking.


Another.
S MAster, may I speak a little to you?


M Say on.


S We two have propounded if you think so good, to walk abroad while the rest
do play.


M Whither will you go?


S In the next suburb.


M But what will ye do as you are walking?


S We will discourse (handle some dialogue).


M But it must be of some good and honest matters.


S This clearnes of the season, and the beautiful face of the earth, will
afford us an honest subject.


M There never wants matter to prais God, only it must be by his faithful
servants.


S Truly never. But to return to the matter propounded, does it please you
master to permit us to go without the city?


M Unless your constant fidelity, and true love of learning were known to me, I
would never suffer it: espetially for that wicked youths have often deceaved me
in this way. Go ye therefore hence, and come again* betimes to supper.


An other.
S MAy it pleas you to give me leave to go out?


M Whither?


S Home.


M How is it that you go so often home?


S My mother commanded that I and my brother should come to her this day.


M For what cause?


S That our maid may beat out our clothes.


M What is that to say? Are you louzie?


S Yes very louzie.


M Wherefore have you not told my wife of it?


S we dare not.


M You speak as if she were so chur^ish, she keeps a maid principally for that
cause, that she may take care to keep you all clean, and you know that well
enough, but you are glad when you can have an oppertunity to visit your mother.
Tarry therefore, to morrow I will take care that your clothes shall be beaten out.


S But our mother will chide us.


M I will satisfy her. Be you still.


6 Eenige korte t'samen sprekingen waerinne Scholejongens van haren meester
verlof eyschen om verscheydene oorsaecken uyt te gaen.
S IS het my gheoorloft uyt te gaen met mijn Broeder?


M Wat is de oorsaeck?


S Dat onse moeder ons schoenen koopt: daerna dat wy aae de barbier gaen.


M Wat daer?


S Om ons hayr af te snijden.


M Wat is dat nu van noode?


S Op dat wy morghen (so het de Here sal toeghelaten hebben) onsen Oom
versoecken.


M Gaet end brengt morgen vroegh even ghetuyghenisse often even ghetuyge met u.


S Met God's hulp sal ick dat naerstelijck versorghen; wilt ghy noch jets mere
meesier?


M Dat ghy van mijnent weghen u moeder ghedienstelijcken groet.


Even ander.
S IS het geoorloft buyten te gaen meester?


M Waer henen?


S Eerst na de snyer / daerna tot de barbier.


M Waerom tot de snyer?


S Dat ick mijn koussen te vermaken besorghe.


M Sijn se ghescheurt?


S Also ghescheurt / dat ickse nauwelijcx kan aentrecken.


M Waerom tot de barbier?


S Op dat ick hem toone even gezweer / het welcke my desen dagh op mijn dye
ontstaen is.


M Ontdeckt het / op dat ick het sie.


S Siet het wanneer 't u belieft.


M Het is even puysken.


S So gisten ick.


M Als ghy 't ontdeckt sult hebben / so begeert dan dat hy even bequame
pleyster daer op leyt.


S Ick sal done dat ghy my raet.


M Maer isser oock niemandt die met u gaen will?


S Ia / Ioannes Fluvianus.


M Wat heeft hy te done?


S Hy will oock na de Barbier gaen.


M Gaet dan to samen / end keert te ghelijck wederom.


S Wilt ghy yets meerder?


M Dat ghy u haest weder te komen / op dat ghy u achternoenmael niet en
verliest.


Even ander.
S MEester / isset geoorloft even weynigh te spreecken?


M Spreeckt.


S Wy twee stelden voor / so het u alsoo goet dunckt / buyten te gaen wandelen
/ terwijl de andere speelen.


M Waer wilt ghy lieden henen gaen?


S In de naeste hooren.


M Maer wat sult ghy al wandelende done?


S Wy sullen eenige t'samensprekinghe handelen.


M Maer van goede end eerlijcke dinghen.


S Dese he^detheydt des tijts / end so schoonen gelaet des aerdrijcx sullen
ons even eerlijcken inhoudt gheven.


M Daer en ontbreect nimmermeer stoffe om Godt te loben / alleen door sijne
ware dienaers.


S Nimmermeer voorwaer: maer op dat wy tot onse voorghestelde reden weder
keeren / laet ghy het ons toe meest er buyten de Stadt te gaen?


M Ten waer dat my bekent waer uwe geduerighe ghetrouwicheydt end ware liefde
tot de talen / ick en soude het nimmermeer toelaten voornamentlijck nadien
loose jonghelinghen my dickmaels in dese maniere bedroghen hebben. Ghy lieden
dan gaet heenen en keert broegh wederom tot het avontmael.


Even ander.
S IS het niet gheoorloft uyt te gaen?


M Waer henen?


S Na huys toe.


M Hoe so dickwils t'huys te gaen?


S Mijn moeder had gheboden dat ick end mijn broeder op huyden tot haer souden
gaen.


M Om wat oorsaecks will?


S Om dat de meyt onse kleederen soude uyt-cloppen.


M Wat is dat te segghen? hebt ghy lieden luysen?


S Iae seer veel.


M Waerom hebt ghy 't mijn huysbrouw' niet vermaent?


S Wy durben niet.


M Al even eens of sy so stuers waer / sy heeft even meydt aldaermeest om die
oorsaer will / op dat sy sorghe draeght voor uwer allen reyndelijckheydt / end
ghy lieden weet dat well / maer ghy zijt blijde dat u gheleghen theydt ghegheven
wordt om u moeder te versoecken. Ghy lieden blijft dan / morgen sal ick
versorghen dat uwe kleederen uytgheklopt werden?


S Maer onse moeder sal'ons berispen.


M Ick sal haer versoenen, Rust u. l,


7 A Scholar inviteth his master in his fathers name to walk to the garden.
S GOd save you master.


M You are well come, what message have you?


S My father desires that we may go together on pleasure into his garden
without the city.


M The fairnes of the weather inviteth us thereunto, and we have now leisure,
but what shall we see there that is pleasant to be looked upon?


S Diverse and fair trees with their fruits, also a wonderful variety of
herbs and flowers.


M There is nothing more pleasant than those things.


S That is the bounty of God toward us.


M Which we must prosecute with continual praises.


S But I fear that we make my father tarry for us.


M Tarry so long till that I put off my gown, that I may be the fitter to
walk. now I am ready, let us go, but is your father at home?


S He tarryed for us at the door.


M That Is well: See that you salute him decently.


S We have bin often warned of that by your teaching.


7 Even School-jonghen noodight sijn meester uyt naem van sijn Vader in den hof
te gaen.
S ZYt ghe^roet Meester.


M Met ghe'uck moet ghy ghekomen zijn / wat voorschapt ghy?


S Mijn Vader bid dat wy t'samen om geneught / in sijn tuyn / buyten de Stadt
moghen gaen.


M Tot die saeck noodight ons de helderheydt des weers / end wy hevben nu tijt
/ maer wat sullen wy daer s^en gheneuglijcx om te aenschouwen?


S Verscheyden end schoone boomen met hare vruchten item even wondere
verscheydenheyt van cruyden end bloemen.


M Daer en is niet geneugelijcker als die dinghen.


S Dat is de mi'daedigheydt Godts teghen ons.


M Die wy met gedurigen lof moeten navolghen.


S Maer ick breese dat wy mijn vader naer ons done wachten.


M Wacht so lang tot dat ic mijn tabbaert verandere / op dat ick te veerdiger
zy om te wandelen. Nu ben ick bereydt / laet ons gaen* maer is u vader t'huys?


S Hy wacht ons voor de deur.


M Die saeck is goet: Siet dal ghy hem bequamelijth groet.


S Daer van zijn wy door u leeringhen dickmaels vermaent gheweest.


8 The diligence of a learned father in teaching of his children at home.
N WHen came you again from home?


O I came but even now.


N Where is your brother?


O He stays at home.


N Why does he tarry?


O That he might dine with his mother.


N But wherefore did you not tarry also?


O I had dyned before with my father.


N Who served you?


O The maid.


N What did your mother? where was she?


O At home also, but busy.


N About what business?


O In receaving wheat, which was brought to us.


N when shall you go home again?


O When I shall be sent for by my father.


N On what day will that be?


O It may be four days hence.


N Wherefore go you home so often?


O Our parents will have it so.


N What do ye at home?


O That which our parents bid us.


N But in the mean time your time of learning is lost with you.


O It is not altogether lost.


N What then?


O As often as my father is not very busy, he exercises us at all hours, in
the morning, before and after dinner, before supper, long enough after supper,
lastly also before we go to bed.


N In what things does be exercise you?


O He asks of us principally those things which we learned the whole week at
school, he looks into our theams, and questioneth us from the same: Often
times does he give us something to write out sometimes in latin sometimes in
our mothers tongue, Sometimes also be propounds to us a short sentence in our
mothers tongue, which we translate into latin: Sometimes on the other hand he
bids us translate some thing out of latin into our mothers tongue. Lastly
before and after meat, do we read something out of the English or French bible
and that in the presence of the whole family.


N Does he ask you nothing of the Catechism?


O He does that every Sunday, unles perhaps he be from home.


N You tell me wonderful things if they be but true.


O Yea there are many more than I have told you: For I have forgotten the
civility of manners, of which he uses also to admonish us at the table.


N Why does your father take so much pains to teach you?


O That so he may understand whither in the school we do cast away his
labor, and mispend the time.


N Wonderful diligence and providence of man! Oh how much are you bound to
your heavenly father, who has given you such a father!


O God grant, that we may never forget that and al his other benefits.


N It is a good and godly thing to wish that: Take heed that you have it not
only in your mouth, but more also in your heart.


O I thank you, that you admonish me so faithfully.


N We owe the duty of good admonition to all men, but most of all to our
brethren.


O Do we then owe that to our brethren allone?


N I call them chiefly brethren here, who are made one with us by faith in
Christ.


O You judge well. But I will go and see whither my brother he also come from
home at last for he is more than too ready to loyter.


8 De neersticheyt van even geleerden Vader in it onderwijsen van sijn kinderen
binnens huys.
N WAnneer zijt ghy weder van huys ghekomen?


O Ick kom nu eerst wederom.


N Waer is u broeder?


O Hy is t'huys ghebleven.


N Waerom is hy ghebleven?


O Op dat hy met sijn moeder het middachmael soude houden.


N Maer ghy warom zijt ghy oock niet ghebleven?


O Ick / had mijn middachmael al ghehouden met mijn vader.


N Wie dienden u?


O De meyd.


N Wat deed u moeder? waer was die?


O Oock t'huys / maer besigh


N In wat saeck?


O In tarw te ontfanghen dit ons toeghevoert werde.


N Wanneer sult ghy weder na huys keeren?


O Als ick van mijn vader ontboden sal worden.


N Op wat dach sal dat zijn?


O Moghelijck over vier deghen?


N Waerom gaet ghy lieden so dickmaels t'huys?


O So willen 't onse ouders.


N Wat doet ghy lieden t'huys?


O Dat ons van onse ouders bevolen werdt.


N Maer ondertusschen gaet by u de tijt der leeringen verloren.


O Sy en gaet niet gheheel verloren.


N Wat dan?


O So menighmael mijn Vader niet noodsaecke ijck besigh is / so oeffent hy ons
tot allen uren smorgens / voor end nae het middachmael / voor het avontmael /
nae het avontmael langh ghenoegh / eyndelijck oock eer wy te bed gaen.


N In wat dinghen oeffent hy u?


O Hy vereyscht van ons aldermeest die dinghen die wy de gantsche weeck in het
school gheleert hebben / hy siet in onse themata / end vraeght ons van de
selve: dickwils gheeft hy ons nu yets in Latijn often in onse moeders tael uyt
te schrijven / somtijts ste dt hy ons oock voor even korte spreucke in onse
moeders tael / die wy in het Latijn oversetten somtijts daerenteghen ghebiet hy
yets latijns in onse moeders tael oversetten. Eyndelijck voor end nae den eten
/ lesen wy altijts yet uyt de Engelsche of Fransche Bijbel end dat in het
bijwesen van het geheele hursghesin.


N En vraeght hy niet van de Catechismus?


G Dat doet hy all Sondagh ten sy dat hy misschien van huys zy.


N Ghy vertelt wonderlijcke dinghen / soose maer waerachtich zijn.


O Iae daer zijnder noch veel mere als dien ick u vertelt heb: want ick heb
vergheeten de beleeftheyt der manieven / van de welcke hy ons oock pleeg te
vermaenen over de taeffel.


N Waerom neemt u Vader so veel arbeyt om u te leeven?


O Op dat hy also verstae / of wy in het School sijn arbeyt verdoen / end de
tijdt misbruycken.


N Wonderlijcke neersticheyt end voorsichtigheydt des menschen! O hoe seer
zijt ghylieden verbonden aen den Hemelschen vader / die u sodanighen vader
ghegheven heeft!


O Godt geve dat wy dat end sijne andere weldaden nimmermeer vergheten.


N Het is goedt end Godt bruchtig dat te wenschen Maeckt dat ghy het niet
alleen inde mondt hebt / maer oock noch meerder inder harten.


O Ick bedanck u dat ghy my so ge trouwelijc vermaent.


N Den dienst van well te vermanen zijn wy schuldigh aen all menschen maer
aldermeest aen onse Broeders.


O Sijn wy dat dan aen onse broeders alleen schuldich?


N Ick noem die hier voornamelijck broeders / die uyt het gheloove in Christum
met ons vereenight zijn.


O Ghy oordeelt well. Maer ick ga sien of mijn broeder ten laetsten oock weder
van huys gekomen is: want hy is mere dan al te veerdigh om te vertoeven.


9 Two boys compare their age together. The diligence of a learned pedagogue is
praised.
P HOw old are you?


Q Thirteen years, as I have understood from my mother: but how old are you?


P Truly I am not so old.


Q How old then?


P I want one year of it.


Q You are then twelve.


P That account is ready.


Q But your brother, what year does he go in?


P In his fifth.


Q what say you? He speaks latin already.


P Why do you marvel? we have always apedagogue at home, who is both
learned and diligent, he teaches us always to speak Latin, and speaks
nothing in our mothers tongue except to expound something: also we dare speak
none other to our father but latin.


Q Do you then never speak English?


P Only with our mother, and that at a certain bou^e when she bids us come to
her.


Q What do you with the household?


P We have seldom speech with the household, and that only as they go by: and
yet the men servants themselves speak to us in latin.


Q What do the mayds?


P When occasion is offered that we speak to them, then we use our mothers
tongue, like as we use with our mother herself.


Q Oh happy are ye, who are so diligently instructed.


P God be thanked, by whose grace we have a father, that takes care to have
us so well taught.


Q Truly the prais and glory of that matter belongs only to the heavenly
Father.


P But what do we? I hear the roll called.


Q Let us then make have.


9 Twee jonghens vergelijcken hare jaeren. De naerstigheydt van even gheleerde
kindermeester were gepresen.
P HOe veel jaren hebt ghy?


Q Derthien / ghelijck ick van mijn moeder verstaen hebbe maer ghy?


P Voorwaer ick hebber so veel niet.


Q Hoe veel dan?


P Daer ontbreeckter even.


Q So sijnder dan twaelf.


P Die rekeningh is veerdich.


Q Maer u Broeder / in sijn hoe veelste jaer gaet hy?


P In sijn vijfste.


Q Wat seght ghy? hy spreect nu al Latijn.


P Wat verwondert ghy u? altijt hebben wy t'huys even kinder-meester die
gheleert end neerslich is / hy leert ons altijdt latijn spreecken / oock en
seydt hy in onse moeders tael niet ten sy om yet te verklaren: oock en derven
wy onsen Vader niet dan in 't Latijn aenspreecken.


Q Spreeckt ghy lieden dan nimmermeer Engelsch?


P Alleen met onse moeder end dat op even seker ure als sy ons ghebiedt tot
haer te komen.


Q Wat doet gh^lieden met het huysghesin?


P Met het huysgesin hebben w^ seldom reeden / end oock alleen in h^t voorb^
gaen: end nochtans de knechten selfs spreecken ons in 'r Latijn aen.


Q Wat done de meyskens?


P So wanneer het te pas komt dat w^ haer aenspreecken / so gebruycken wy onse
moeders tael / ghelijck wy met onse moeder selfs pleghen.


Q O ghy gheluckighe die so naerstighlijck onderwesen werdt!


P Godt zy danck door wiens gratie wy even Vader hebben / die versorght dat wy
so fraey geleert werden.


Q Voorwaer den lof end de eere van die saeck komt alleen den Hemelschen Vader
tee.


P Maer wat done wy? ick hoor de naem-rollen nu al opsegghen


Q Laet ons dan haesten.


10 Two boys count their books. Men must read extraordinary books sometimes. It
is better to learn late than never. A book that is finely bound is showed. Some
questions about it. we must not provoke God in our jests.
R HAve you many books also?


S Not v^ry many.


R But which have you?


S The grammar rudiments, the school dialogues Terence, Tullies epistles,
Cato, a dictionary, the high dutch testament, the Psalms with a catechism
besides a writing book to write out the masters dictates. But what books have
you?


R I have all them which you reckoned except Cato, Terence, and Tully's
epistles. For wherefore should I have books that are not read in our School?


S But I read those sometimes when we are at leisure, that I may always take
on some new thing espetially in the latin tongue and good manners.


R You do wisely my Solomon. O wretch that I am, who have never yet learned,
what it is to be studious.


S Learn therefore for it is better to learn late than never.


R But what is that new gilded book which you show so stately?


S A Terence.


R Where was it printed?


S At Paris.


R Who gave it you?


S I bought him with my money.


R Where got you the money?


S You ask that foolishly, as if I had stolen it.


R Far be it from me to think so, but I asked that for my mynds sake.


S Neither do I reprove your words in good earnest: but we are wont to jest so
with our good friends.


R Jesting is not forbidden, if God be not provoked thereby. But go to, let
us return to our purpose. Of whom did you buy that Terence?


S Of Clement.


R Of that peddler?


S Yes indeed.


R How much did it cost?


S Ten styvers.


R No more at all.


S Not at all.


R Truly a price small enough.


S Espetially seeing it is gilt and bound so finely.


R were there other such like books also?


S Two or three.


R Conduct me I pray thee to him.


L Let us go.


10 Twee jonghens tellen malckanders boecken. Men moet somtijts onghewoone
boecken lese^. Het is beter te laet als nimmermeer te leeren. Even boeck dat
fraey gebonden is werdt vertoont. Eenighe vraeghen daer over. In onse
boertinghen moetmen Godt niet vertoornen.
R Hebt ghy oock veele boecken?


S Niet seer veel.


R Maer welcke hebt ghy?


S De beginselen van de grama^ica / d^ Schoo^sche t'samen-spreekinghen /
Terentius / de brieven van Cicero / Cato / even dictionarium / het hooghduytsche
testament / de psalmen met even Catechismus / daerenboken even schrijf boeck / om
de uytlegginghen des meesters in te schrijven. Maer ghy wat boecken hebt ghy?


R Ick hebse al die ghy getelt hebt behalven Cato / Terentius / end de brieven
van Cicero. Want waerom soude ick boecken hebben die in onse School niet
boorgeleesen en werden?


S Maer ick lees die somtijts terwijl wy lecdich zijn / om dat ick altijt wat
nieuws aennee^e / voornaementlijck inde latijnsche tael end de goede zeden.


R Ghy doet voorsichtigh mijn Solomon. O mijn ellendige die nimmermeer gheleert
hebbe wat het zy vlytigh te zijn.


S Leert dan / want het is beter te laet als nimmermeer te leeren.


R Maer wat is dat voor even nieuw vergult boeck dat ghy so heerlijck vertoont?


S Even Terentius.


R Waer ghedruckt?


S Te Parijs.


R Wie heeft u die ghegeven?


S Ick hebt him voor mijn gelt ghekocht.


R Van waer hadt ghy het gelt ghekreghen?


S Sottelijc vraeght ghy dat / al even eens of ick het gestoolen had.


R Dat zy verre van my dat te dencken: maer ick vraeghde dat om de gheneught.


S Nochte ick en berispe u woorden niet ernstelijc: maer op die manier pleeghen
wy te spotten met onse goede vrienden


R Niet verbieter te jocken als Godt maer niet vertoornt en werde. Maer well aen
/ laet ons tot onse voorneemen wederkeeren: van wien hebt ghy dien Terentius
ghekocht?


S Van Clement.


R Van dien Kramer?


S Iae voorwaer.


R Hoe veel heeft hy gekost?


S Thien sluyvers.


R Niet met al mere?


S Gantsch niet.


R Voorwaer even prijs slecht genoech.


S Voornamenslijck dewijl het vergult end so fraey gebonden is.


R Waerender oock andere diergelijcke boecken?


S Twee often dry.


R Geleyt my bid ick tot hem.


S Laet ons gaen.


11 All things ought to be set after heavenly matters. It is shameful for one
to reprove another of that whereof he himself is guilty. A father gives the
master charge of his son. Correction is necessary, and therefore to be borne.
The commendations of Scholasticall discipline. A rare example of a boy that
desires of himself to dwell in the School.
T Wherefore were you not at the sermon to day?


V I was busy in writing letters.


T Could you not defer that business?


V The have of the Car^ er forced me.


T But the master teaches us that we must set all things after Gods matters.


V He teaches it indeed, neither do I make dout of it: but we are never so
perfect, but we often set God after earthly things.


T That is naught.


V Yea very evil but we are always men, until God do change us by his
spirit. But tell me (I pray thee) was the auditory full?


T Not very full, according to the wonted manner.


V Whereby comes that?


T Know you not that the people are now busy in gathering grapes?


V I know it well: but can not men spend one hour in divine matters?


T It belongs not to me to give you a reason of that; Only this I say, It is
a shame to a teacher when the fault does reprove himself again.


V Ho: what a blow have thou given me on the ch^ek! Farewell, I will not add a
word more.


T Be more circumspect then an other time. But I heard even now that your
father came to school to day.


V You have heard the truth.


T For what cause I came he?


V To pay the master money for my diet: and with one to give him charge of me.


T Had he never given him charge of thee?


V Yes very often.


T What mean then so manyfold admonitions?


V He loves me truly.


T What ^hen?


V Therefore he desires that I may be taught diligently.


T What if he give charge that you should be often beaten?


V It may be that is the cause, but what of that? he does not therefore love
me the less.


T whence do you draw that?


V Because correction is so necessary for a child as food.


T Indeed you speak true: but few judge so. Also there is no body but he would
rather have bread than the rod.


V That is natural to everyone, who denies it? but yet men must beat
punishment patiently, espetially when it is just.


T This sentence is in the little book of manners: Remember to bear patiently
that which you suffer justly. But what if the punishment be unjust?


V We must bear that also nevertheles.


T For whose sake?


V For Jesus Christ, who has suffered an unjust and cruel death for our sins.


T Oh that that came into our mynds so often as we suffer any thing.


V The master does often teach us that, so often as the occasion serves: but
he knocks at a deaf man's door as the proverb i^.


T Let us then do our endeavor that we may be more diligent hereafter.


V God grant it.


T But do you not know what came into my mind a while since?


V What is it, I pray?


T I think to betake my self to the School.


V what in the school? to dwell?


T Not that I would dwell there as an hireling, but that I may learn with you
at the master's table.


V O would to God that you spoke that heartily and truly.


T By our mutual acquaintance and friendship you ought suffitiently to
understand, that I am not wont to feign or dissemble any thing with you.


V I understood that plainly long since, but when I heard that word from you,
my affection caught me into that exclamation, so wholly was I forgetful of my
self.


T I take your speech none otherwise, but to the purpose, My father truly does
not compel me: but I perceave well enough by his countenance and words that it
pleases him very well.


V Namely that is, because your father being a very prudent man knows well,
that liberal wits will not be compelled, but will be easily led. But yet I
doubt not but that it happens by the instinct of Gods Spirit. But what do
you think?


T I am of the same mind: espetially since that I am bent thereto of mine own
accord.


V It is a great argument, that that counsel is of God.


T I believe that, for they who are compelled to that thing by their parents,
do for the most part seek refuges (excuses) by which they may avoid the
command.


V To speak the truth, I have found it sometimes in my self, namely before I
entered therein. For what think thou? I heard of those slaves of Satan, so
many evil speeches what of the master, and what of the discipline, that me
thoughts I rather went into a prison or a bakehous than into a school. If you
also should fall into such a plague, it is no marvail if he do with hands and
feet and al his power attempt to terrify you from so holy a purpose.


T I have not yet made it known to any body.


V what you say to me is not to make it known.


T I know it well enough, but that happened to me out of Terence, let us now
proceed to the things which remain.


V What remains, but that you pray to God, and that you go forth manfully
with your purpose?


T You shall hear of your daily diet at your table, of the familiarity of the
master and ussher in hearing the repetition of the lessons, of ye masters
gentleness while we are eating together, of that friendly strife of the
learning after supper, of the freedom of speaking of honest things when there
is leisure time, of the free reproof which is among you. I say of all these
things you have told me enough at an other time, all which pleas me very well:
yet me thinks there is nothing more profitable than that continual exercise of
speaking latin; For what greater fruit is there of study? what more honest?
what more pleasant? espetially where one does freely reprove another without
hatred or envy.


V What is in that, that the conquered in the strife of those things are only
punished with shame, and the conquerours rewarded with a pr^ze?


T In a word, all your exercises do delight me marvelously, by the very
hearing of them.


V How much more would you say that, if you saw those things, if you were
present when they are don?


T I will God willing be present.


V would to God it might be shortly.


T I hope so truly: But there remains one scruple. whereof you can easily free
me if you will.


V I will do it in good truth if I am able, but in the mean time take b^ed
that you be not too inquisitive, as we have heretofore seen in Terence. Go
to, tell me at length what that difficulty is?


T I have evil thoughts of your Scholastical discipline, not yt I will
therefore desist from my begun work: but that when I have heard of that matter
I may come the more cheerfully and pleasantly.


V Unless you were one who (as I know) esteem me neither vain nor a liar, I
would verily be rather wholly silent of that, than tell those things to you
which I conceave.


T why so?


V Because it is difficult to make those things seem true, albeit they are
otherwise very true, so long as ye Common people speak and judge very evil
things of them.


T why so, I pray?


V For by the lies of the ungodly our discipline sounds so ill among those
idiots, that it is a wonder that any one will dwell in our school: Nevertheles
(which is the work of God) how worse name we have, so many the more come to us.


T There is no need of so long an introduction: Tell me all things plainly,
and fear not: you shall not terrify me. I have understood all things, and
pondered them in my mind before.


V Attend therefore: I will briefly tell all that seems to be of any worth.
First I would that you did persuade your self this that the master is much more
courteous to us than in the school be makes show of before us. For he
converseth so familiarly with us, as a wise father uses to do among his
children. But you will say then, why is he so severe in public? I answer,
because that without such a severity (as I have heard from himself when he once
told it to a good friend) so great an heap of scholars endued with so many
different fashions, can neither be kept under, nor held to their bounden duty,
for every one would live after his own fashion, after his own mind.


T Besides that I have by my self often admired that there is in the School
such great reverence, so great silence, lastly so great modesty.


V You would wonder much more, if you had ever seen the Country schools.


T I have seen them sometimes, and have dil^gently considered them. There is
more quietness in our school of six hundred, than of forty children, yea
thirty in those petty schools. But go forward I pray thee; I fear that you
will be an Asian Orator for your speech begins already to digres too far.


V But you do interrupt my speech your self.


T I have spoken nothing besides the purpose: but go on now.


V Shall I tell you in a word? This domestical discipline although it seems
odious to a few ungodly ones, yet it very well likes the good and diligent
children for the profits sake. For if our matters were loose at home what
should we have then against the assaults of those wanton and saucie fellows?
what rest should we have? what stilnes of our studies? Therefore the discipline
it self is to the true lovers of study a refuge and as it were a bulwark, none
otherwise than as an haven is to seamen in a storm. To conclude, whosoever
among us is peaceable, and always ready to do well, he is preserved by the
discipline. For the master does not busy himself to draw us by stripes to the
study of learning: but he rather indeavoureth to bring us thereto most chiefly
by these things, by an honest and new entertaynment, by good will, Courtesy,
and gentleness of manners, by benefits, and lastly by the love of virtue and
learning, whence it comes to pas, that the greatest part of us are so affected
that we study how to obey him heartily.


T Otherwise ye would not discharge the bounden duty of scholars.


V But there are some knaves, who fear neither God, nor their parents, nor
stripes who do also hate study worse than a dog and snake that I may use the
words of Horace: Against such (I say) severe discipline is some times used;
namely because necessity constrains.


T I have enough. For now I see to what and the severity of your discipline
tends.


V Namely, that it may defend good manners, but either to amend or drive out
the bad.


T Truly I do salute that discipline very much, so far is it from me to fear
it. And I love you my Valentine for this freedom of speech of those things,
whereby you have given me sharper provocations.


V But I give immortal thanks to our heavenly father who has given you a good
mind and sincere judgment.


T Farewell then, and expect me the next week if God permit. Again farewell,
and earnestly commend the work which we have begun unto God in your prayers.


V I wish you a quiet night, and sound sleep.

V Och often eerst daeghs.

V Om dat het swaer is die dinghen waerschijnelijck te maken / hoewel sy

onderhouden.
